Then this speaker said: If the act and the object are analogous to hitting and the hit, we say: it is inconceivable that hitting exists without the hit, so it must be inconceivable that creation exists without the created. So we say to him in reply: How do you distinguish yourself from one who says to you: If creation and the created are not analogous to hitting and the hit, then it should be permissible for creation to exist without the created? And if hitting is not permissible without the hit, the summary is that we claim the necessity of distinction between that which is an act of the essence and that which is its object, and we argue by the visible, while you say it is impossible for an act without an object and you argue by the visible. Each claims the falsehood of the other's statement, because he claims about the unseen what contradicts the visible. Then there is no doubt that equivalence between the visible and the unseen is obligatory when equivalence is obligatory, and differentiation between them is obligatory when differentiation is obligatory. For we all equated between the visible world and the unseen world in the affirmation of knowledge, and likewise in the other attributes, and we differentiated between the one who wills what is foolish and ugly like cursing himself and other than that in the visible and the unseen due to the necessity of differentiation. Then here we equated in the act and the object between the visible and the unseen, and we said that their distinction is necessary in both the visible and the unseen because of the evidence of equivalence, which is what has passed: that the act is an effecter and the object is affected, so their distinction is necessary by inferring from power and the object of power. And this meaning exists in the unseen, and for this reason equivalence was made between the visible and the unseen regarding the necessity of distinction between power and the object of power. And you affirm differentiation here between the visible and the unseen, so you say: The act, even if it is other than the object in the visible, yet in the unseen the act should be the object even if the evidence of equivalence stands. And we differentiated in this between the visible and the unseen, and we said: Creation without a created is permissible, and hitting without a hit is impossible, because the evidence necessitates differentiation. And that is because the act is the turning of the possible from possibility to necessity. For it is possible that Zayd be in the house, and possible that he not be. So if he is outside the house, his being in the house is possible, since the possible is that which is not established in the present, and if its existence is assumed, no impossibility follows from it. So when he enters, then the being which was in the state of possibility, i.e., conceivability and permissibility, becomes necessity, i.e., actuality and existence. And likewise this is the case in everything that is an act. Then among its conditions is the occurrence of the turning of the possible from possibility to necessity in general, so that it is a turning and not its accompaniment. However, the act in the visible is an accident. So if the turning of the possible by it from possibility to necessity did not occur, its occurrence afterwards would not be conceivable, because the act, as soon as it exists, perishes in the next moment, and the non-existent cannot be conceived as having influence on the affected. So the hitting occurring from Zayd which befell Amr was an act of his, because it turns the possible pain in Amr from possibility to necessity, and it was hitting because it is a turning of a specific possible, namely pain. So if the hitting existed from Zayd while Amr was non-existent, the survival of the hitting until the time of Amr's existence in order for pain to occur in Amr by it is inconceivable, due to the impossibility of its survival because it is an accident, so it perishes immediately. Then Amr exists but there is no hitting, so no pain occurs in him. Thus, the existence of hitting without a hit was not conceivable. As for the act in the unseen, it is necessarily perpetual because it is eternal like the other attributes, so it remains until the time of the existence of the object, and by it occurs the turning of this possible from possibility to necessity, so it was an act from the beginning. An analogy of this in judicial matters is killing, for it is an act due to which the soul departs from the body of the animal. So if the shooting occurs from a human then from the arrow, and it hits the target and wounds him, then pains follow successively, then his soul departs due to that.
Source pages p. 534

Commentary
This passage is an extended rebuttal of an opponent's argument that divine action (' فعل') must be simultaneous with its object (' مفعول'), by analogy with created actions like hitting (' ضرب') and the hit (' مضروب'). The author demonstrates that the analogy fails because of a crucial difference: created actions are temporal accidents that perish instantly, while divine action is eternal and subsists.

The passage begins by presenting the opponent's claim: if action and object are like hitting and the hit, then just as hitting cannot exist without a hit, creation cannot exist without a created thing. The author responds by challenging the opponent: how would you reply to someone who says that if creation is not analogous to hitting, then creation without a created thing is permissible? The core dispute is whether action and object must be distinct (مغايرة) or can be identical. Both sides argue from the visible world (شاهد) to the unseen (غائب), but each accuses the other of inconsistency.

The author then establishes a principle: analogy between the visible and unseen is valid only when the evidence demands equivalence; when evidence demands differentiation, it must be made. He cites examples: we affirm knowledge and other attributes equally in both realms, but we differentiate in cases like willing something foolish – in the visible, a wise person would never will such, but in the unseen, we affirm that God can will anything. Similarly, regarding action, the author argues that the evidence of the visible (that an act effects change in its object) requires distinction between act and object in both realms. The opponent, however, insists on a difference: in the visible, act and object are distinct, but in the unseen, act and object should be identical, despite the evidence of equivalence. The author counters that he has differentiated based on evidence that necessitates differentiation.

The key to the argument is the nature of an act: it is 'turning the possible from possibility to necessity' (صرف الممكن من الإمكان إلى الوجوب). An act takes something that could be or not be and brings it into existence. In the visible, acts are accidents (أعراض) that perish instantly. For a temporal act like hitting to have an effect, the act and object must exist simultaneously, because the act does not endure to affect a later object. If Zayd hits Amr, the hitting occurs at the same time as the pain; it cannot precede. Therefore, in the visible, an act without its object is impossible. But in the unseen, divine acts (like creation) are eternal (أزلي) and subsisting. They can exist before their objects and then, when the object is created, the act brings about the effect. Thus, creation without a created is possible because the creative act is eternal and persists until the moment of creation.

The author provides a judicial analogy: killing (قتل) involves a sequence – the shooting, the arrow hitting, wounds, pain, and finally death. The initial act (shooting) is considered the cause of death even though death occurs later. This mirrors the divine act: it precedes its object and remains effective.

In summary, this passage defends the Ash'ari/Maturidi position that divine attributes and acts are distinct from their objects and that temporal analogies do not fully apply to the eternal realm.

Key Terms
مغايرة
مؤثر
متأثر
قدرة
مقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent's claim that act is identical to object in the unseen is based on the same evidence as the author's claim.
Correction: The author argues that evidence from causality actually supports distinction, not identity.

Checkpoint
What evidence does the author use to argue for the necessity of distinction between act and object in both realms?
A) The act is eternal while the object is temporal.
B) The act is an effecter and the object is affected.
C) The visible world requires it by consensus.
D) The opponent agrees in the visible.

Key Takeaway
Causal relationship between act and object necessitates their distinction in all realms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that every act of yours is distinct from its object and effect, how would this change the way you evaluate your own deeds? Are you ready to accept that the outcome of an action is not the same as the action itself, and that only Allah's power creates the effect?
?
Think about a recent situation where you felt your effort directly caused a result. According to this lesson, is that belief accurate? What would it mean for your reliance on Allah if you consistently distinguished your act from its object?
?
The author insists on equivalence between visible and unseen realms. Is your faith in Allah's actions and attributes consistent with what you observe in the world? Are there any areas where you secretly treat the unseen realm differently, and what would it cost you to align your thinking?

Temporal vs Eternal Acts: Turning Possibility into Reality
Author explains act as turning possible to necessary and differentiates temporal from eternal acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why temporal acts require simultaneous objects while eternal acts can precede their objects, based on the definition of an act as turning the possible into necessary.

Core Idea
Temporal acts perish instantly; eternal acts subsist and can effect later.

The author now provides the core metaphysical argument that resolves the apparent contradiction. An act is defined as turning a possible thing from possibility to necessity (bringing it into existence). In the visible world, acts are accidents (أعراض) that vanish the moment they exist. For an accident to affect something, it must coincide with its object. But divine acts in the unseen are eternal and persistent, so they can exist before their object and still cause it to come into being. This is illustrated with the example of Zayd entering a house and then a judicial analogy of killing.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of act
Read the definition: an act is the turning of a possible from possibility to necessity. The example of Zayd entering the house illustrates this.
2
Compare temporal and eternal acts
Notice that temporal acts are accidents that perish instantly. They cannot affect a non-existent object because they don't last.
The hitting example shows this: if Amr doesn't exist when the blow occurs, the blow vanishes before he appears.
3
Understand eternal acts
Divine acts are eternal and persist. They can remain until the object exists and then turn the possible into actual.
This is why creation without a created is possible in the unseen.
4
Consider the judicial analogy
The killing analogy shows how a sequence of events can be attributed to an initial act that precedes the effect.
The shooting is considered the act even though death occurs later.

Commentary

The author demonstrates that the analogy fails because of a crucial difference: created actions are temporal accidents that perish instantly, while divine action is eternal and subsists.

He cites examples: we affirm knowledge and other attributes equally in both realms, but we differentiate in cases like willing something foolish – in the visible, a wise person would never will such, but in the unseen, we affirm that God can will anything. The author counters that he has differentiated based on evidence that necessitates differentiation.

The initial act (shooting) is considered the cause of death even though death occurs later. This mirrors the divine act: it precedes its object and remains effective.

In summary, this passage defends the Ash'ari/Maturidi position that divine attributes and acts are distinct from their objects and that temporal analogies do not fully apply to the eternal realm.

The Principle and the Distinction Between Visible and Unseen
Nasafi states the principle and defines act as turning possibility into necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational Maturidi principle that the act is considered the object acted upon, yet a distinction must be drawn between the visible and unseen worlds regarding whether an act can exist without its object.

Core Idea
Acts in the visible world are transient; in the unseen they are eternal.

The author begins by stating a key principle: the act should be identified with its object even when evidence suggests otherwise. However, he immediately introduces a crucial distinction between the visible world (shahid) and the unseen world (gha'ib). This distinction is the foundation for the entire argument that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial statement
Read the first sentence carefully. It states that the act is the object acted upon, but then introduces a differentiation between shahid and gha'ib. Note the examples of creation without a created thing and striking without a struck thing.
This sets up the problem that the rest of the lesson will explore.
2
Understand the definition of act
The author defines an act as the turning of a possible thing from possibility (imkan) to necessity (wujub). Follow the example of Zayd entering the house to see how a possible state (being in the house) becomes actual.
This definition is essential for understanding why acts in the visible world cannot persist.
3
Identify the condition for an act
Note the condition: the act itself must be the turning, not merely coexisting with the turning. This clarifies that the act is defined by its transformative effect.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينبغي أن يكون الفعل هو المفعول وإن قام دليل التسوية، وفرقنا نحن في هذا بين الشاهد والغائب، وقلنا بجواز الخلق، ولا مخلوق وامتناع الضرب ولا مضروب لأن الدليل يوجب التفرقة، وذلك لأن الفعل صرف الممكن من الصفات، فيبقى إلى وقت وجود المفعول فيحصل به صرف هذا الممكن من الإمكان إلى الوجوب فكان فعلا من الأصل، ونظير هذا في الحكميات القتل فإنه لما كان فعلا تنزهق بسببه الروح عن بدن الحيوان، فإذا وجد الرمي من إنسان ثم من السهم فأصاب المرمى إليه وجرحه ثم ترادفت الآلام ثم انزهقت روحه بسبب ذلك، يظهر عند انزهاق روحه أن ذلك الرمي من حين وجد كان قتلا وإن لم يوجد للحال أثره في المحل وجد القتل ولا مقتول، ولهذا يظهر تعلق أحكام القتل بذلك حتى إن القتل لو كان خطأ فكفر كفارة القتل جاز، وإن كان المرمى إليه بعد حيا فكذا هذا، وفي فعل الشاهد لا يتصور حصول أثره الحقيقي بعد انعدامه، ففرقنا بين الشاهد والغائب لقيام دليل التفرقة. وبهذا فرقنا نحن والأشعرية في وجود الأمر ولا مأمور بين الشاهد والغائب وجوزنا ذلك في الغائب وقلنا بإحالته في الشاهد فعين هذا الفرق، فتذكرت الأشعرية هذا الفرق هناك ونسيت ها هنا. وجاء من هذا كله أن سوينا في الفعل والمفعول في إيجاب القول بتغايرهما بين الشاهد والغائب لقيام دليل التسوية، وفرقنا فيهما في حق جواز انفكاك أحدهما عن الآخر بين الشاهد والغائب لقيام دليل التفرقة. والأشعرية قلبت هذه القصة فسوت في ذلك بين الشاهد والغائب عند قيام دليل التفرقة وفرقت بينهما عند قيام دليل التسوية وهذا قلب للمعقول وخروج عن قضية الدليل، والله الموفق. فإذا سلّمت هذه الطريقة وضح الاستدلال بالضرب والمضروب والقتل والمقتول وسائر الأفعال، واندفعت الأسئلة بحمد الله ومنه. والذي يحقق مذهبنا ويوجب بطلان مذهب الخصوم: أن العالم كان معدوما وكذا كان جزء من أجزائه على التعين، وكان يجوز عليه الوجود فكان العدم متحققا
It should be that the act is the object acted upon even if the evidence of equivalence stands, and we differentiate in this between the visible world and the unseen world, and we said the permissibility of creation without a created thing and the impossibility of striking without a struck thing because the evidence necessitates differentiation. That is because the act is the turning of the possible from possibility to necessity. For it is possible that Zayd be in the house, and it is possible that he not be. If he is outside the house, his being in the house is possible, since the possible is that which is not actual in the present. If its existence is assumed, no impossibility follows from it. If he enters, then the state of being (al-kawn) that was in the limit of possibility, i.e., conception and permissibility, turns to necessity, i.e., realization and existence. And so this is in everything that is an act. Then one of its conditions is the occurrence of the turning of the possible from possibility to necessity in general so that it is a turning, not merely a coexistence. However, the act in the visible world is an accident; if the turning of the possible by it from possibility to necessity did not occur, its occurrence afterwards would not be conceivable, because the act, as soon as it exists, perishes in the next moment, and the non-existent cannot be conceived to have an effect upon something affected. Thus the striking that occurs from Zayd and falls upon `Amr is an act of his, because it turns the possible pain in `Amr from possibility to necessity, and it is striking because it is a turning of a specific possible, namely pain. If the striking from Zayd exists and `Amr is non-existent, it is inconceivable that the striking persist until the time of `Amr's existence so that the pain occurs in `Amr by it, because its persistence is impossible since it is an accident; it perishes immediately, then `Amr comes to exist and there is no striking, so no pain occurs in him. Thus the existence of striking and a struck thing is inconceivable. As for the act in the unseen world, it is necessarily perpetual because it is eternal like the other attributes, so it remains until the time of the existence of the object acted upon, and by it occurs the turning of this possible from possibility to necessity, so it was an act from the beginning. An analogy to this in legal matters is killing: since it is an act by which the spirit departs from the body of the living being, if the throwing occurs from a human then from the arrow, and it hits the target and wounds him, then pains follow successively, then his spirit departs because of that, it becomes apparent at the time of his spirit's departure that that throwing was killing from the moment it occurred, even if its effect was not immediately present in the location. The killing exists without a killed person. For this reason, the rulings of killing apply to it, such that if the killing was accidental, the expiation of killing is permissible, even if the target was still alive afterwards. So this is the case. In the act of the visible world, it is not conceivable that its real effect occurs after its cessation, so we differentiated between the visible and the unseen due to the existence of evidence of differentiation. And by this we and the Ash`aris differentiated in the existence of a command without a commanded person between the visible and the unseen, and we permitted that in the unseen and said it is impossible in the visible, so this is the same distinction. The Ash`aris remembered this distinction there but forgot it here. From all this it follows that we equated between the visible and the unseen regarding the act and the object acted upon in necessitating the statement of their distinctness due to the existence of evidence of equivalence, and we differentiated between them regarding the permissibility of one being separated from the other between the visible and the unseen due to the existence of evidence of differentiation. The Ash`aris reversed this story: they equated in that between the visible and the unseen when the evidence of differentiation existed, and differentiated between them when the evidence of equivalence existed. This is an inversion of reason and a departure from the requirement of evidence, and God is the Granter of success. If this method is accepted, the argument by striking and the struck, killing and the killed, and all acts becomes clear, and the questions are repelled by the praise and grace of God. What confirms our doctrine and necessitates the invalidity of the opponents' doctrine is that the world was non-existent, and likewise every part of it specifically, and existence was possible for it, so non-existence was actual.
Source pages p. 535

Commentary
This passage is a sustained kalam argument that addresses the relationship between an act (fi'l) and its object (maf'ul) in the visible world (shahid) versus the unseen world (gha'ib). The author, Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, defends the Maturidi position against the Ash'ari position by establishing a distinction based on the nature of acts as accidents in the visible world and as eternal attributes in the unseen world. The argument proceeds as follows: First, he states the general principle that the act should be considered the object acted upon (i.e., the act is defined by its effect), but a distinction must be made between shahid and gha'ib. In the visible world, an act like striking cannot exist without a struck object because the act is an accident that perishes instantly; if the object does not exist at the moment of the act, the act cannot persist to affect it later. To illustrate this, he uses the example of Zayd entering a house: the possibility of Zayd being in the house turns to actuality when he enters. The act (entering) is the turning of a possibility into necessity. However, an accident (like striking) ceases to exist immediately, so if the recipient (Amr) does not exist at the time of the strike, the strike cannot produce pain later because it no longer exists. Hence, striking without a struck person is impossible in the visible world. In contrast, in the unseen world (gha'ib), divine acts are eternal and perpetual like other attributes; they persist until the object comes into existence, so the act can exist without its object at the time of the act. For example, God's act of creation existed before the world existed, and it brought about the world when it was created. Nasafi then draws an analogy to legal killing: if someone shoots an arrow that hits and kills later, the killing act (the shooting) is considered killing from the start even though the victim was alive at the time. This is because the act (the shooting) is not an accident that perishes; it is a sequence of events, and the ruling of killing applies based on the eventual effect. In the visible world, such a case would require the act to persist, but accidents cannot persist. Thus, the distinction is necessary. The author then criticizes the Ash'aris for inconsistently applying this distinction: they accept it in the case of command and commanded (amr wa ma'mur) but reject it here. He concludes that his method clarifies all such cases and refutes the opponents. The passage ends by affirming the Maturidi doctrine that the world was non-existent and then came into existence, which is impossible without a creator whose act is eternal.

Key Terms
الفعل (act)
المفعول (object acted upon)
الشاهد (visible world)
الغائب (unseen world)
الإمكان (possibility)
الوجوب (necessity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is an act?
A) A mere event in time
B) The turning of a possible from possibility to necessity
C) The coexistence of agent and patient
D) A substance with duration

Key Takeaway
The act is defined as the transformation of a possibility into actuality, and this definition underlies the Maturidi distinction between visible and unseen worlds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that every act is a transformation from possibility to necessity. Think of a decision you made today—something as simple as what you wore or said. In that moment, did you realize that you were turning a possibility into a fixed reality that cannot be undone? How does this awareness change your view of the weight of your choices? What will you do today to ensure your acts are turning possibilities toward what pleases Allah?
?
The passage distinguishes between the visible and unseen worlds when it comes to acts. In your own life, you often see only the outcomes of people's actions, not the inner act of will that caused them. How often do you judge others based solely on what you see, forgetting that the true act is the inner transformation of possibility into intention? Will you commit to pausing before judging, remembering that Allah alone sees the hidden act?
?
Nasafi uses the example of Zayd entering a house: before entering, it was possible he would be inside; after entering, it becomes necessary. Reflect on a time when you were in a state of possibility—perhaps unsure about a belief or action—and then you made a choice that solidified it. Are you living in a state of passive possibility, or are you actively turning possibilities into commitments to truth? What is one area of your faith where you need to move from 'possible belief' to 'necessary conviction' today?

Why Striking Without a Struck Object is Impossible in the Visible World
Nasafi explains why striking without a struck object is impossible in the visible world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, in the visible world, an act like striking cannot exist without its object, due to the transient nature of accidents.

Core Idea
Accidents perish instantly; they cannot persist to affect a later object.

Having defined an act as the turning of possibility into necessity, the author now applies this to the visible world. He argues that acts in the visible world are accidents (a'rad) that cease to exist immediately. If the object does not exist at the moment of the act, the act cannot persist to produce its effect later. This explains why striking without a struck person is impossible in the visible world.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition for an act
The author repeats the condition: the act itself must be the turning, not just coexisting. Then he states that in the visible world, acts are accidents.
2
Follow the argument about accidents
Read why accidents cannot persist: they perish in the next moment. Therefore, if the effect is not immediate, the act cannot cause it later.
Key point: non-existent things cannot have effects.
3
Analyze the striking example
The example of Zayd striking Amr shows that if Amr does not exist at the time of the strike, the strike cannot cause pain later because the strike disappears immediately.
This proves the impossibility of striking without a struck object.

الإمكان إلى الوجوب، فإن من الممكن أن يكون زيد في الدار، ومن الممكن أن لا يكون، فإذا كان خارج الدار كان كونه في الدار ممكنا إذ الممكن هو أن لا يكون ثابتا في الحال، وإذا فرض وجوده لم يلزم منه محال، فإذا Commentary
This passage is a sustained kalam argument that addresses the relationship between an act (fi'l) and its object (maf'ul) in the visible world (shahid) versus the unseen world (gha'ib). The author, Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, defends the Maturidi position against the Ash'ari position by establishing a distinction based on the nature of acts as accidents in the visible world and as eternal attributes in the unseen world. The argument proceeds as follows: First, he states the general principle that the act should be considered the object acted upon (i.e., the act is defined by its effect), but a distinction must be made between shahid and gha'ib. In the visible world, an act like striking cannot exist without a struck object because the act is an accident that perishes instantly; if the object does not exist at the moment of the act, the act cannot persist to affect it later. To illustrate this, he uses the example of Zayd entering a house: the possibility of Zayd being in the house turns to actuality when he enters. The act (entering) is the turning of a possibility into necessity. However, an accident (like striking) ceases to exist immediately, so if the recipient (Amr) does not exist at the time of the strike, the strike cannot produce pain later because it no longer exists. Hence, striking without a struck person is impossible in the visible world. In contrast, in the unseen world (gha'ib), divine acts are eternal and perpetual like other attributes; they persist until the object comes into existence, so the act can exist without its object at the time of the act. For example, God's act of creation existed before the world existed, and it brought about the world when it was created. Nasafi then draws an analogy to legal killing: if someone shoots an arrow that hits and kills later, the killing act (the shooting) is considered killing from the start even though the victim was alive at the time. This is because the act (the shooting) is not an accident that perishes; it is a sequence of events, and the ruling of killing applies based on the eventual effect. In the visible world, such a case would require the act to persist, but accidents cannot persist. Thus, the distinction is necessary. The author then criticizes the Ash'aris for inconsistently applying this distinction: they accept it in the case of command and commanded (amr wa ma'mur) but reject it here. He concludes that his method clarifies all such cases and refutes the opponents. The passage ends by affirming the Maturidi doctrine that the world was non-existent and then came into existence, which is impossible without a creator whose act is eternal.

Key Terms
الفعل (act)
المفعول (object acted upon)
الشاهد (visible world)
العرض (accident)
الضرب (striking)
المضروب (struck person)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an act like striking can be considered an act even if the object never exists, because the act is defined by the agent alone.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the act is defined by its effect (turning possibility into necessity). Without an object to receive the effect, no turning occurs, so the act cannot exist. In the visible world, the transient nature of accidents makes it impossible for the act to wait for the object.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi argue that striking without a struck person is impossible in the visible world?
A) Because striking is a substance that requires a location
B) Because the act of striking is an accident that perishes instantly, so it cannot affect an absent object
C) Because the struck person must be present to give consent
D) Because the evidence of equivalence forces it

Key Takeaway
In the visible world, acts are transient accidents; therefore, they cannot exist without their objects at the moment of occurrence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Nasafi argues that your acts are transient accidents—they perish as soon as they occur. Your good intentions do not cause real change unless they coincide with the object's existence. Is there a good deed you have been postponing, thinking your intention is enough? What specific act will you perform today to truly turn a possibility into reality for someone else?
?
Your life is a series of vanishing events, each perishing like a strike without a struck object if not directed properly. What is the ultimate object of your actions? Are you living each moment as if it is the only chance to make that object real—or are you assuming your acts will persist without the object?
?
When you sin, it is a past act—gone like an accident. But Nasafi shows that the effect (pain) is also transient. In the afterlife, however, deeds are recorded and persist. Does this fleeting nature of your actions make you feel their weight more or less? Are you living with the urgency that every act is a turning of possibility into necessity that cannot be undone?

Acts in the Unseen World: Eternal and Persistent
Nasafi argues that acts in the unseen world are eternal and can thus precede their objects, using the killing analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why acts in the unseen world (like divine creation) can exist without their objects at the time of the act, because they are eternal and persistent.

Core Idea
God's acts are eternal attributes; they remain until the object comes to be.

Having shown that acts in the visible world cannot be separated from their objects, the author now turns to the unseen world. He argues that divine acts are not transient accidents; they are eternal attributes that persist. Therefore, God's act of creation existed before the world and caused it to come into being. The killing analogy further illustrates how an act can be considered effective even if its full effect is delayed.

Guided Path
1
Read about acts in the unseen world
The author states that acts in the unseen world are necessarily perpetual (wajib al-dawam) because they are eternal like other attributes. They remain until the object comes into existence.
Contrast this with the transient nature of accidents in the visible world.
2
Understand the killing analogy
The example of killing shows that the act of shooting is considered killing from the moment it occurs, even though death happens later. This is because the act is not a transient accident but a sequence that leads to the effect.
The rulings of killing apply immediately, showing that the act is considered 'killing' even before the death occurs.
3
Connect to the distinction
The author concludes that this evidence necessitates the differentiation between the visible and unseen worlds.

ينبغي أن يكون الفعل هو المفعول وإن قام دليل التسوية، وفرقنا نحن في هذا بين الشاهد والغائب، وقلنا بجواز الخلق، ولا مخلوق وامتناع الضرب ولا مضروب لأن الدليل يوجب التفرقة، وذلك لأن الفعل صرف الممكن من الإمكان إلى الوجوب، فإن من الممكن أن يكون زيد في الدار، ومن الممكن أن لا يكون، فإذا كان خارج الدار كان كونه في الدار ممكنا إذ الممكن هو أن لا يكون ثابتا في الحال، وإذا فرض وجوده لم يلزم منه محال، فإذا دخل فقد صار الكون الذي كان في حد الإمكان أي التصور والجواز إلى الوجوب أي التحقق والوجود، وكذا فهذا في كل ما هو فعل، ثم من شرطه حصول انصراف الممكن من الإمكان إلى الوجوب في الجملة ليكون هو صرفا لا مقارنته إياه، إلا أن الفعل في الشاهد عرض فلو لم يحصل انصراف الممكن به عن الإمكان إلى الوجوب لما تصور حصوله بعده، لأن الفعل كما وجد ينعدم في الثاني، والمعدوم لا يتصور منه التأثير في المتأثر، فكان الضرب الحاصل من زيد الواقع على عمرو فعلا له، لأنه يصرف الألم الممكن في عمرو من الإمكان إلى الوجوب، وكان ضربا لكونه صرفا للممكن مخصوص وهو الألم، فلو وجد الضرب من زيد وعمرو منعدم لا يتصور بقاء الضرب إلى وقت وجود عمرو ليحصل به الألم في عمرو، لاستحالة بقائه لكونه عرضا فينعدم للحال ثم يوجد عمرو ولا ضرب فلا يحصل فيه الألم، فلم يتصور وجود الضرب ولا مضروب فأما الفعل في الغائب فواجب الدوام لكونه أزليا كسائر الصفات، فيبقى إلى وقت وجود المفعول فيحصل به صرف هذا الممكن من الإمكان إلى الوجوب فكان فعلا من الأصل، ونظير هذا في الحكميات القتل فإنه لما كان فعلا تنزهق بسببه الروح عن بدن الحيوان، فإذا وجد الرمي من إنسان ثم من السهم فأصاب المرمى إليه وجرحه ثم ترادفت الآلام ثم انزهقت روحه بسبب ذلك، يظهر عند انزهاق روحه أن ذلك الرمي من حين وجد كان قتلا وإن لم يوجد للحال أثره في المحل وجد القتل ولا مقتول، ولهذا يظهر تعلق أحكام القتل بذلك حتى إن القتل لو كان خطأ فكفر كفارة القتل جاز، وإن كان المرمى إليه بعد حيا فكذا هذا، وفي فعل الشاهد لا يتصور حصول أثره الحقيقي بعد انعدامه، ففرقنا بين الشاهد والغائب لقيام دليل التفرقة. وبهذا فرقنا نحن والأشعرية في وجود الأمر ولا مأمور بين الشاهد والغائب وجوزنا ذلك في الغائب وقلنا بإحالته في الشاهد فعين هذا الفرق، فتذكرت الأشعرية هذا الفرق هناك ونسيت ها هنا. وجاء من هذا كله أن سوينا في الفعل والمفعول في إيجاب القول بتغايرهما بين الشاهد والغائب لقيام دليل التسوية، وفرقنا فيهما في حق جواز انفكاك أحدهما عن الآخر بين الشاهد والغائب لقيام دليل التفرقة. والأشعرية قلبت هذه القصة فسوت في ذلك بين الشاهد والغائب عند قيام دليل التفرقة وفرقت بينهما عند قيام دليل التسوية وهذا قلب للمعقول وخروج عن قضية الدليل، والله الموفق. فإذا سلّمت هذه الطريقة وضح الاستدلال بالضرب والمضروب والقتل والمقتول وسائر الأفعال، واندفعت الأسئلة بحمد الله ومنه. والذي يحقق مذهبنا ويوجب بطلان مذهب الخصوم: أن العالم كان معدوما وكذا كان جزء من أجزائه على التعين، وكان يجوز عليه الوجود فكان العدم متحققا
It should be that the act is the object acted upon even if the evidence of equivalence stands, and we differentiate in this between the visible world and the unseen world, and we said the permissibility of creation without a created thing and the impossibility of striking without a struck thing because the evidence necessitates differentiation. That is because the act is the turning of the possible from possibility to necessity. For it is possible that Zayd be in the house, and it is possible that he not be. If he is outside the house, his being in the house is possible, since the possible is that which is not actual in the present. If its existence is assumed, no impossibility follows from it. If he enters, then the state of being (al-kawn) that was in the limit of possibility, i.e., conception and permissibility, turns to necessity, i.e., realization and existence. And so this is in everything that is an act. Then one of its conditions is the occurrence of the turning of the possible from possibility to necessity in general so that it is a turning, not merely a coexistence. However, the act in the visible world is an accident; if the turning of the possible by it from possibility to necessity did not occur, its occurrence afterwards would not be conceivable, because the act, as soon as it exists, perishes in the next moment, and the non-existent cannot be conceived to have an effect upon something affected. Thus the striking that occurs from Zayd and falls upon `Amr is an act of his, because it turns the possible pain in `Amr from possibility to necessity, and it is striking because it is a turning of a specific possible, namely pain. If the striking from Zayd exists and `Amr is non-existent, it is inconceivable that the striking persist until the time of `Amr's existence so that the pain occurs in `Amr by it, because its persistence is impossible since it is an accident; it perishes immediately, then `Amr comes to exist and there is no striking, so no pain occurs in him. Thus the existence of striking and a struck thing is inconceivable. As for the act in the unseen world, it is necessarily perpetual because it is eternal like the other attributes, so it remains until the time of the existence of the object acted upon, and by it occurs the turning of this possible from possibility to necessity, so it was an act from the beginning. An analogy to this in legal matters is killing: since it is an act by which the spirit departs from the body of the living being, if the throwing occurs from a human then from the arrow, and it hits the target and wounds him, then pains follow successively, then his spirit departs because of that, it becomes apparent at the time of his spirit's departure that that throwing was killing from the moment it occurred, even if its effect was not immediately present in the location. The killing exists without a killed person. For this reason, the rulings of killing apply to it, such that if the killing was accidental, the expiation of killing is permissible, even if the target was still alive afterwards. So this is the case. In the act of the visible world, it is not conceivable that its real effect occurs after its cessation, so we differentiated between the visible and the unseen due to the existence of evidence of differentiation. And by this we and the Ash`aris differentiated in the existence of a command without a commanded person between the visible and the unseen, and we permitted that in the unseen and said it is impossible in the visible, so this is the same distinction. The Ash`aris remembered this distinction there but forgot it here. From all this it follows that we equated between the visible and the unseen regarding the act and the object acted upon in necessitating the statement of their distinctness due to the existence of evidence of equivalence, and we differentiated between them regarding the permissibility of one being separated from the other between the visible and the unseen due to the existence of evidence of differentiation. The Ash`aris reversed this story: they equated in that between the visible and the unseen when the evidence of differentiation existed, and differentiated between them when the evidence of equivalence existed. This is an inversion of reason and a departure from the requirement of evidence, and God is the Granter of success. If this method is accepted, the argument by striking and the struck, killing and the killed, and all acts becomes clear, and the questions are repelled by the praise and grace of God. What confirms our doctrine and necessitates the invalidity of the opponents' doctrine is that the world was non-existent, and likewise every part of it specifically, and existence was possible for it, so non-existence was actual.
Source pages p. 535

Commentary
This passage is a sustained kalam argument that addresses the relationship between an act (fi'l) and its object (maf'ul) in the visible world (shahid) versus the unseen world (gha'ib). The author, Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, defends the Maturidi position against the Ash'ari position by establishing a distinction based on the nature of acts as accidents in the visible world and as eternal attributes in the unseen world. The argument proceeds as follows: First, he states the general principle that the act should be considered the object acted upon (i.e., the act is defined by its effect), but a distinction must be made between shahid and gha'ib. In the visible world, an act like striking cannot exist without a struck object because the act is an accident that perishes instantly; if the object does not exist at the moment of the act, the act cannot persist to affect it later. To illustrate this, he uses the example of Zayd entering a house: the possibility of Zayd being in the house turns to actuality when he enters. The act (entering) is the turning of a possibility into necessity. However, an accident (like striking) ceases to exist immediately, so if the recipient (Amr) does not exist at the time of the strike, the strike cannot produce pain later because it no longer exists. Hence, striking without a struck person is impossible in the visible world. In contrast, in the unseen world (gha'ib), divine acts are eternal and perpetual like other attributes; they persist until the object comes into existence, so the act can exist without its object at the time of the act. For example, God's act of creation existed before the world existed, and it brought about the world when it was created. Nasafi then draws an analogy to legal killing: if someone shoots an arrow that hits and kills later, the killing act (the shooting) is considered killing from the start even though the victim was alive at the time. This is because the act (the shooting) is not an accident that perishes; it is a sequence of events, and the ruling of killing applies based on the eventual effect. In the visible world, such a case would require the act to persist, but accidents cannot persist. Thus, the distinction is necessary. The author then criticizes the Ash'aris for inconsistently applying this distinction: they accept it in the case of command and commanded (amr wa ma'mur) but reject it here. He concludes that his method clarifies all such cases and refutes the opponents. The passage ends by affirming the Maturidi doctrine that the world was non-existent and then came into existence, which is impossible without a creator whose act is eternal.

Key Terms
الغائب (unseen world)
الأزلية (eternality)
القتل (killing)
الرمي (throwing)
الروح (spirit)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that all acts, including divine acts, require an immediate object, similar to physical striking.
Correction: Nasafi shows that divine acts are eternal and not accidents; they can persist and thus precede their objects. The killing analogy illustrates that even in human legal contexts, an act can be considered effective before its full effect occurs.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi say that acts in the unseen world can exist without their objects?
A) Because they are substances with duration
B) Because they are eternal and persist until the object exists
C) Because the object is also eternal
D) Because the evidence forces us to equate them

Key Takeaway
Divine acts in the unseen world are eternal and persistent, allowing them to exist without their objects initially.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that God's acts are eternal and persist even when their effects are not yet seen. Where in your life are you waiting for a divine act to manifest? Does knowing that the act is already complete in God's decree change how you wait? What is one specific patience you will adopt today because of this truth?
?
The killing analogy shows that an act can be judged by its inevitable consequence. Think about a habit or action you have that you know will lead to a harmful consequence. Are you living as if that consequence is already written? How can you change your daily choices to align with the reality that Allah sees the end from the beginning?
?
Nasafi differentiates between the unseen and visible worlds based on evidence. In your faith, have you ever allowed your limited vision of the visible world to undermine your certainty in the unseen? Ask yourself: What visible discomforts or delays are causing me to doubt the reality of Allah's eternal acts? Make a firm intention to trust His timing today.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
7 min
Application to Command and Critique of Ash'aris
Nasafi applies the distinction to command and criticizes Ash'ari inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how the same distinction applies to the issue of command (amr) and commanded (ma'mur), and understand the inconsistency of the Ash'ari position.

Core Idea
Ash'aris inconsistently accept the distinction for command but reject it for act and object.

Having established the distinction between shahid and gha'ib for the act-object relationship, the author now extends the same reasoning to another key theological issue: God's command and the commanded person. He points out that the Ash'aris themselves accept that God's command can exist without a commanded person in the unseen, but they reject the analogous case for act and object. This inconsistency, Nasafi argues, reveals the weakness of the Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Read the comparison to command
The author says that Maturidis and Ash'aris both differentiated between shahid and gha'ib for the existence of a command without a commanded person. They allowed it in the unseen and forbade it in the visible.
2
Identify the Ash'ari inconsistency
Nasafi accuses the Ash'aris of remembering this distinction in the case of command but forgetting it here (act and object). This is the core critique.
3
Understand the reversal
Maturidis used equivalence (taswiya) for the distinctness of act and object, and differentiation (tafriqa) for their separability. Ash'aris reversed this, which is irrational.
4
Read the conclusion
If this method is accepted, all arguments become clear, and the opponents' doctrine is invalidated.

Commentary
This passage is a sustained kalam argument that addresses the relationship between an act (fi'l) and its object (maf'ul) in the visible world (shahid) versus the unseen world (gha'ib). The author, Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, defends the Maturidi position against the Ash'ari position by establishing a distinction based on the nature of acts as accidents in the visible world and as eternal attributes in the unseen world. To illustrate this, he uses the example of Zayd entering a house: the possibility of Zayd being in the house turns to actuality when he enters. The act (entering) is the turning of a possibility into necessity. However, an accident (like striking) ceases to exist immediately, so if the recipient (Amr) does not exist at the time of the strike, the strike cannot produce pain later because it no longer exists. Hence, striking without a struck person is impossible in the visible world. For example, God's act of creation existed before the world existed, and it brought about the world when it was created. Nasafi then draws an analogy to legal killing: if someone shoots an arrow that hits and kills later, the killing act (the shooting) is considered killing from the start even though the victim was alive at the time. This is because the act (the shooting) is not an accident that perishes; it is a sequence of events, and the ruling of killing applies based on the eventual effect. The author then criticizes the Ash'aris for inconsistently applying this distinction: they accept it in the case of command and commanded (amr wa ma'mur) but reject it here. He concludes that his method clarifies all such cases and refutes the opponents. The passage ends by affirming the Maturidi doctrine that the world was non-existent and then came into existence, which is impossible without a creator whose act is eternal.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Ash'ari position on act and object is consistent with their position on command.
Correction: Nasafi shows that Ash'aris inconsistently apply the same distinction: they accept it for command but reject it for act and object.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what error did the Ash'aris commit?
A) They denied that God's command can exist without a commanded person
B) They reversed the application of evidence of equivalence and evidence of differentiation
C) They claimed that all acts are eternal
D) They argued that striking without a struck object is possible in the visible world

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi position consistently applies the distinction between visible and unseen to both act-object and command-commanded, unlike the Ash'aris.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Nasafi says the Ash'aris 'remembered this distinction there but forgot it here.' Are there areas in your life where you apply one standard of consistency to your beliefs about Allah but a different standard to your actions? What is one specific inconsistency you will work to correct this week?
?
The passage calls the Ash'ari reversal 'an inversion of reason.' When you encounter a truth that challenges your comfortable assumptions, do you invert reason to protect your pride? Will you commit to submitting your intellect to the evidence, even if it is uncomfortable?
?
Nasafi ends with 'والله الموفق' (God is the Granter of success). Do you genuinely believe that true intellectual consistency in matters of faith is a gift from Allah, not just human effort? How will you humbly ask Him for this gift today?

Refutation of Attaching Existence to Capacity Without Creation
Al-Nasafi rejects the sufficiency of capacity for existence and critiques the Karamiyya's metaphorical usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi argues that God's capacity (qudra) alone is insufficient to bring things into existence and how this differs from the Karamiyya's error.

Core Idea
Capacity makes something possible, not actual; creation is a separate act.

Al-Nasafi confronts an objection that would reduce divine creation to mere capacity. He shows that capacity (qudra) only entails possibility, not existence, and that confusing the two leads to absurd consequences. He then contrasts this with the milder error of the Karamiyya who misuse metaphorical language.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how al-Nasafi first states an objection and then refutes it.
The opening 'لأن' might be a fragment; the passage is a continuous argument.
2
Identify the two refutations
Al-Nasafi gives two reasons against capacity alone producing existence: first, capacity only makes something 'capable' not existent; second, if it did, God would not need to be called Creator. Locate both in the text.
3
Compare with the Karamiyya error
Al-Nasafi says the Karamiyya's error is about expressions (metaphor vs reality), while the main error is about realities. Understand the distinction.
This highlights the difference between a theological mistake in concepts vs. a linguistic one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن ولا يقال إنه اختص بالوجود بعد العدم بمعنى هو غيره وهو قدرة الباري، القدرة تقتضي كون ما يدخل تحتها مقدورا أما لا يقتضي كونه موجودا، ولو اقتضى كونه موجودا لكان إيجادا إذ الإيجاد وما يوجب الوجود كالإجلاس وما يوجب الجلوس وليس المقدور بموجود لا محالة ، ولهذا يوصف المعدوم بأنه مقدور فدل أن هذا باطل، ولأن الموجود لو حصل بالقدرة لم يكن بنا حاجة إلى القول بالخلق والإيجاد فكان الله تعالى قادرا على العالم لا خالقا له(٢) ولا موجدا، ويظهر بطلان هذا القول ظهور بطلان هذا السؤال، والله الموفق. ثم جهالة من يعلق الوجود بالقدرة دون الإيجاد أفحش من جهالة الكرامية (٣) حيث يثبتون اسم الخالق بالقدرة دون صفة الخلق لأن ذلك من باب العبارات (٤)، وقد يوجد مثل ذلك في الشاهد وإن كان مجازا فجهالتهم أنهم ظنوا المجاز حقيقة، وهؤلاء علقوا() بحقيقة القدرة ما لا يصلح حكما لها، فصاروا كمن علق الوجود بالعلم أو السمع أو البصر، وذلك محال فكذا هذا، وجعلوا الإيجاد والتكوين عبارتين فارغتين عن المعنى. فإذا هاتان جهالتان في الحقائق، وتلك جهالة واحدة في محال العبارات.
Because it cannot be said that it was specified with existence after non-existence by a meaning other than it, and it is the capacity of the Creator. Capacity entails that what falls under it is capable, but does not entail that it exists. If it entailed its existence, it would be creation, for creation and what necessitates existence are like sitting and what necessitates sitting. And the capable is not necessarily existent. For this reason, the non-existent is described as capable. So this indicates that this is false. And because if the existent were obtained by capacity, we would have no need to speak of creation and bringing into existence. Then God would be capable of the world but not its creator nor its bringer-into-existence. And the falsity of this statement appears as clearly as the falsity of this question. And God is the grantor of success. Then the ignorance of one who attaches existence to capacity without creation is more coarse than the ignorance of the Karamiyya, who affirm the name of Creator by capacity without the attribute of creation, because that is a matter of expressions. Such may be found in the witnessed even if metaphorically. So their ignorance is that they thought the metaphor is reality. And these people attached to the reality of capacity what is not suitable as a judgment for it. So they became like one who attaches existence to knowledge or hearing or sight, and that is impossible. So likewise this. And they made 'bringing into existence' and 'originating' empty expressions devoid of meaning. So these are two ignorances regarding realities, and that is one ignorance regarding the domain of expressions.
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by a further criticism. Al-Nasafi first raises the objection that the specifier could be God's capacity (qudra). He refutes it by arguing that capacity only entails that something is capable (maqdur), not that it exists. If capacity necessitated existence, it would be identical to creation (ijad), which is absurd. Moreover, if existence came from capacity alone, God would be called Qadir (capable) but not Khaliq (creator). He then compares this view to that of the Karamiyya, who affirm the name 'creator' based on capacity alone without the attribute of creation—a metaphorical usage. The real error is attaching existence to capacity, which is like attaching existence to knowledge or sight. Thus, those who do this commit two ignorances: one regarding realities (attaching existence to capacity) and one regarding expressions (the Karamiyya's metaphorical confusion).

مقدور
موجود
معدوم
كرامية
مجاز
حقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some hold that God's capacity (qudra) alone is sufficient to bring things into existence without a separate act of creation.
Correction: Capacity only makes something capable of existing (maqdur); actual existence requires a distinct act of creation (ijad). Confusing the two leads to absurdities like denying God's attribute of creation.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Nasafi reject the idea that God's capacity alone brings about existence?

B) Because capacity only makes something possible, not necessarily existent, and if it did, it would be identical to creation.
C) Because the Quran states that God creates through His word.
D) Because the Karamiyya already held this view.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi establishes that capacity (qudra) only makes something possible, not actual, and that confusing it with creation leads to denying God's role as Creator.

?

How does this lesson expose that assumption as a denial of God's role as Creator? What will you do today to remind yourself that capacity does not equal actuality?
?
Al-Nasafi warns against emptying the terms 'bringing into existence' and 'originating' of real meaning. Are there concepts in your faith—like tawakkul, rizq, or qadr—that you have reduced to empty slogans, losing their true weight? What would it cost you to restore their reality in your life?
?
The Karamiyya mistook metaphorical language for literal truth. Where in your own understanding of God's attributes or actions might you be doing the same—taking a metaphor too literally or missing the deeper reality? The Reductio Against Power Alone
Reductio ad absurdum against power alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reductio argument that if existence could come from power alone without a specifier, then all opposites would become interchangeable, leading to absurdity.

Core Idea
If existence arises from power alone, then everything could become its opposite.

Imam al-Nasafi begins by showing the absurd consequences of the assumption that a possible being's existence after non-existence could be due solely to its essence or to power without a distinct specifying agent. He argues that this would imply that all contraries could become each other, thus validating the doctrines of the Dualists and Materialists. Let's examine this logical move carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice how the author constructs the hypothetical scenario.
The passage starts with 'و الوجود ممكنا'.
2
Identify the conditional statement
Find the 'if...then' structure: 'فلو جاز... لجاز'. Understand that the author is supposing a false premise to derive an absurd conclusion.
3
List the examples of opposites
The passage lists several pairs of opposites: separation/mixture, motion/stillness, etc. Note how each would become interchangeable under the false premise.
The argument uses physical and accidental opposites.
4
Understand the conclusion
The reductio leads to affirming the doctrines of the Dualists (ثنوية) and Materialists (دهرية) who deny real change. This is presented as an absurd outcome.
This strengthens the necessity of a specifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجود ممكنا فلو جاز اختصاصه بالوجود الممكن عن العدم المتحقق من غير تخصيص مخصص له بذلك وراء ذاته بل بما هو عين ذاته، لجاز اختصاص ما كان افتراقه متحققا بالامتزاج الممكن من غير معنى وراء ذاته بل هو عين ذاته. وكذا في كل مجتمع افترق أو مفترق اجتمع، وكل متحرك سكن وكل ساكن متحرك وكذا في جميع الأعراض، فيؤدي إلى القول بصحة مذاهب الثنوية ونفاة الأعراض من الدهرية وغيرهم. ولا يقال إنه اختص بالوجود بعد العدم بمعنى هو غيره وهو قدرة الباري، القدرة تقتضي كون ما دخل تحتها مقدورا أما لا يقتضي كونه موجودا، ولو اقتضى كونه موجودا لكان إيجادا إذ الإيجاد وما يوجب الوجود كالإجلاس وما يوجب الجلوس وليس المقدور بموجود لا محالة، ولهذا يوصف المعدوم بأنه مقدور فدل أن هذا باطل، ولأن الموجود لو حصل بالقدرة لم يكن بنا حاجة إلى القول بالخلق والإيجاد فكان الله تعالى قادرا على العالم لا خالقا له ولا موجدا، ويظهر بطلان هذا القول ظهور بطلان هذا السؤال، والله الموفق.
And existence is possible. So if it were permissible that a possible being's specification with existence after verified non-existence occurs without a specification by a specifier other than its essence, but rather by that which is its essence, then it would be permissible for that which is actually separated to be specified with possible mixture without a meaning other than its essence, but rather it is its essence. Likewise in everything that has come together and separated, or separated and come together, and every moving thing to become still, and every still thing to move, and likewise in all accidents. This would lead to affirming the doctrines of the Dualists and the deniers of accidents among the Materialists and others. And it cannot be said that it was specified with existence after non-existence by a meaning that is other than it, namely the power of the Creator, for power necessitates that what falls under it be a thing that is possible, but it does not necessitate that it be existent. If it necessitated that it be existent, it would be bringing into existence (ījād), for bringing into existence and what necessitates existence are like seating and what necessitates sitting, and the possible thing is not necessarily existent. Hence the non-existent is described as possible, which proves that this is false. And because if the existent were obtained by power, we would have no need to speak of creation and bringing into existence, so God would be powerful over the world but not its Creator nor its Originator. The falsehood of this statement appears as the falsehood of this question appears. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by reductio ad absurdum against the idea that existence can be attached to a possible being merely by divine power without a specifying agent distinct from the essence. The argument is: if a possible being's existence after non-existence were due only to its essence (i.e., without a specifier), then the same would apply to all contraries: a thing that is actually separated could be mixed, and vice versa; moving and still could exchange; and all accidents could change arbitrarily. This would validate the doctrines of the Dualists and Materialists who deny real change. The author then anticipates an objection: that the specification is by a meaning other than the essence, namely divine power. The answer is that power necessitates possibility but not existence. If it did, the power would itself be bringing into existence (ījād), but we know that the term 'power' does not entail existence, as non-existents are described as 'possible'. Moreover, if existence came from power alone, there would be no need to speak of creation and bringing into existence (khalq and ījād), reducing God to a mere potential agent rather than the actual Creator. The argument closes by stating the evident falsehood of this position.

Key Terms
وجود
إمكان
اختصاص
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine power (qudra) alone is sufficient to bring something from non-existence to existence without a distinct act of specification.
Correction: The reductio shows that if power alone sufficed without specification, then all opposites would become interchangeable, leading to absurdity. Therefore a specifier (مخصص) is necessary.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would follow if existence could arise from power without a specifying agent?
A) That all possibilities would become actual simultaneously.
B) That the world would be eternal.
C) That contraries could interchange arbitrarily, leading to absurdity.
D) That God would be unable to create.

Key Takeaway
Without a distinct specifier, the existence of a possible being after non-existence would lead to the interchangeability of all opposites, an absurdity that validates false doctrines.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that every event in your life happens by God's mere power without any wisdom or purpose, how would that affect your trust in His promises and your motivation to seek His guidance? What one step can you take today to actively look for His wisdom in a recent event?
?
When you see chaos and disorder in the world, do you still affirm that God's will and wisdom are behind every single occurrence, or do you sometimes doubt? How can you cultivate certainty in His perfect specification, especially when things seem random?
?
The reductio shows that if God acted by power alone without will, the world would be absurd. Are there areas of your life where you act impulsively, without thought or wisdom? How can you imitate God's attribute of wisdom by being more deliberate and reflective in your decisions starting now?

Objection and Refutation on Power
Objection and refutation about divine power as specifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that divine power (qudra) serves as the specifier, and the refutation that power entails possibility, not existence, and would eliminate the need for creation.

Core Idea
Power makes things possible, not existent; otherwise God would be merely powerful, not Creator.

Having established the necessity of an external specifier, Imam al-Nasafi now anticipates an objection: could the specifier be divine power itself? He responds with two key points: power only necessitates that something be possible (مقدور), not that it actually exists. Furthermore, if power directly produced existence, there would be no need to speak of creation (خلق) and bringing into existence (إيجاد). Let's follow this refined argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection states that the specifier is a meaning other than the essence, namely divine power (قدرة الباري). Read this part in Arabic and translation.
The objection begins with 'ولا يقال'.
2
Analyze the first response
The author argues that power necessitates possibility (مقدور) but not existence. If power entailed existence, it would be identical to bringing into existence (إيجاد), but they are distinct.
The comparison with 'seating' (إجلاس) is used as an analogy.
3
Understand the second response
If existence came from power alone, we would have no need for terms like creation (خلق) and bringing into existence (إيجاد). God would be merely powerful, not actually Creator.
This highlights the distinct attribute of takwīn (creation).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author states the falsehood of the objection is as clear as the falsehood of the question. Reflect on why this refutation is necessary for establishing divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refutation of the Shubha About Transitivity of Divine Action
Imam al-Nasafi presents and refutes the doubt about transitivity of divine action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi response refutes the objection that an eternal divine action requires an eternal object.

Core Idea
Eternal action does not necessitate an eternal effect; transitivity implies otherness, not coexistence.

The opponents of the Maturidi position argued that if God's attribute of takwīn (making) is eternal, then its effect—the created world—must also be eternal. In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi presents their doubt and offers a two-part refutation. He first points out that the opponents' own logic proves action and object are distinct, and then illustrates with the example of the divine command.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt carefully
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where the opponent's argument is presented. Note the logical dilemma they propose: if God's act is eternal, its object must either be eternal or non-existent—both problematic.
The opponent tries to force a contradiction between divine eternity and temporal creation.
2
Analyze the first response
In the author's first response, notice how he turns the opponent's own statement against them. The opponent admits that action is transitive to an object, which implies action and object are different. Therefore, coexistence is not required.
This is a clever refutation by accepting the premise and showing it leads to the opposite conclusion.
3
Understand the second response
Read the analogy of the divine command (amr). Just as God's command 'Be' existed eternally without the world existing eternally, so too takwīn can be eternal without its effect being eternal. This clarifies that transitivity does not require simultaneity in all circumstances.
The analogy shows that the 'condition' of transitivity is not absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت بما بينا من الدلائل المغيرة بين التكوين والمكون، فيجب علينا أن نصرف العناية إلى حل ما أوردوا من الشبهات فنقول: قولهم: إن الفعل يتعدى إلى المفعول، وإذا ثبت أنه لا بد أن يتعدى إلى مفعول فلو وجد الفعل في الأزل لكان لا يخلو إما أن كان ما يتعدى إليه موجودا في الأزل أو كان معدما، فإن كان موجودا فهو القول بقدم العالم، وإن كان معدوما ينبغي أن يكون المعدوم مفعولا. الجواب عنه أن نقول: عين هذا الكلام اعتراف منه أن الفعل غير المفعول لأنه زعم أن الفعل يتعدى إلى المفعول، والشيء يتعدى إلى غيره لا نفسه، فإن تعدي الشيء إلى نفسه محال، فإذا هذا الكلام حجة عليه. ثم نقول: الفعل يتعدى إلى المفعول في الجملة، فأما أن يتعدى إليه في الأحوال أجمع فهذا ليس بشرطه، ألا ترى أن الأمر متعد يقال: أمرته فأتمر ثم كان أمر الله تعالى للعالم بالوجود، وهو خطاب كن موجودا في الأزل وكان أمره الذي هو أمر إيجاب موجودا في الأزل ولا مأمور ولا وجوب، فكذا هذا لأنه ما كان أمرا بالوجود ليوجد في القدم بل ليوجد كل موجود في وقت وجوده وما كان أمرا ليجب على المعدوم في القدم بل ليجب على المكلفين وقت وجودهم وبلوغهم مبلغ
And when it is proven by what we have explained of the distinguishing proofs between making (takwīn) and the thing made (mukawwan), it is incumbent upon us to turn our attention to solving the doubts they have raised. So we say: Their statement: 'Action (fiʿl) is transitive to the object of action (mafʿūl). And when it is established that it must be transitive to an object, then if the action existed in eternity, it would be either that what it is transitive to existed in eternity, or it was non-existent. If it existed, that is the statement of the pre-eternity of the world; and if it was non-existent, then the non-existent should be an object of action.' The answer is that we say: This very statement is an admission from him that the action is other than the object of action, because he claims that the action is transitive to the object of action, and a thing is transitive to something other than itself, not to itself; for the transitiveness of a thing to itself is impossible. So this statement is evidence against him. Then we say: Action is transitive to the object of action in general, but that it be transitive to it in all circumstances is not a condition. Do you not see that the command (amr) is transitive: one says 'I commanded him, and he complied.' Then God's command to the world for existence was a speech 'Be, existing in eternity' and His command, which is a command of obligation, existed in eternity, while there was no commanded thing and no obligation. So likewise this, because it was not a command for existence to come into existence in pre-eternity, but rather for every existent to come into existence at the time of its existence, and it was not a command to obligate the non-existent in pre-eternity, but rather to obligate the legally responsible at the time of their existence and their reaching the age of
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage presents a doubt (shubha) from the opponents and the author's answer. The opponents argue: Action (fiʿl) is transitive to its object (mafʿūl). If God's act of making (takwīn) is eternal, then its object must either be eternal (leading to the pre-eternity of the world) or non-existent (which would require the non-existent to be an object of action, which is absurd). The author's answer has two parts. First, the opponent's very statement admits that action is distinct from its object, because one thing cannot be transitive to itself. So the opponent's claim that the object must coexist with the action is false; transitivity implies otherness, not simultaneity. Second, action is transitive in general, but not in every state. The example of divine command (amr) illustrates this: God's command 'Be' existed eternally, yet the command itself is transitive to the commanded thing, but the commanded thing (the world) did not exist in eternity. Similarly, a command of obligation existed eternally without any obligated person. Thus, the transitive nature of divine action does not require the object to be co-eternal. The passage ends abruptly but the core refutation is given.

Key Terms
فعل
مفعول
تعدي
تكوين
أمر
أزل

Checkpoint
What is the author's first response to the opponent's claim that action must be transitive to an object that coexists with it?
A) Action is not transitive at all.
B) The opponent's own admission that action is transitive to something other than itself proves that the action and object are distinct, so coexistence is not required.
C) The object can be non-existent because non-existence is a thing.
D) God's action is unlike human action.

Key Takeaway
An eternal divine action does not require an eternal effect; the transitive nature of action only requires that action and object are distinct, not simultaneous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's command 'Be', does your heart tremble at the fact that His word is eternal, yet you—the commanded—are a temporal creature? How does this change the way you approach His commands in the Qur'an?
?
The opponent's doubt stemmed from limiting God's action to human analogies. Where in your own life have you subconsciously imposed human limitations on God's power or attributes, and what will you do today to correct that?
?
If God's action does not require a co-eternal effect, then His will is utterly sovereign over time. Are there areas in your life where you still act as if God is bound by your timeline or expectations? What step can you take today to submit to His timing?
